        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="63d80cf81abf42d28fa39e92c3e8ea3c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '09bf4475-acb7-4389-b552-4802420bae65'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                     <p>School of Metallurgy and Environment, Engineering Research Center of the Ministry of Education for Advanced Battery Materials, Hunan Provincial Key Laboratory of Nonferrous Value-Added Metallurgy, Central South University, Changsha, 410083 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Jin/Rui">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Lai/Yanqing" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yanqing Lai</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-name">Yanqing Lai</p>
                     <p>School of Metallurgy and Environment, Engineering Research Center of the Ministry of Education for Advanced Battery Materials, Hunan Provincial Key Laboratory of Nonferrous Value-Added Metallurgy, Central South University, Changsha, 410083 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Lai/Yanqing">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zhang/Zhian" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Zhian Zhang<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d264497005" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>To develop the next-generation commercial oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries, it is crucial to reduce the expensive Ni element content, and further regulate redox reaction of cheap transition metal elements such as Mn to elevate specific capacity. Nevertheless, the activation of Mn redox reaction (MRR) remains a challenge, and notably, MRR induces pronounced Jahn-Teller effect, resulting in severe structural distortion and fast performance decay. Herein, activated by Na vacancies and weakened hybridization of O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) orbital, a biphasic low-Ni Mn-based oxide P2/O3-Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2/O3) exhibits reversible MRR, which performs the transition between Mn<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup> during charging and discharging. Due to the interlaced arrangement of P2-type and O3-type crystal domains in P2/O3, the long range Jahn-Teller ordering is restricted to mitigate the cooperative distortion of MnO6 octahedron induced by MRR and the Jahn-Teller effect is suppressed, ensuring sustained stable involvement of MRR in charge compensation. In addition, owing to the introduction of P2-type phase, there is a significant reduction of the migration barrier for sodium ions and no obvious capacity decline after air exposure, leading to a marked enhancement in dynamic performance and air stability of P2/O3, respectively. Consequently, P2/O3 exhibits excellent electrochemical and processing performance.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403955-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403955-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Layered oxide cathodes (LOCs), especially Ni/Fe/Mn-based oxide material, have emerged as the most advanced industrialized cathode candidate for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their mature synthesis processes and outstanding performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0001_R_d264496983e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Mater">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As SIBs industry continues to progress, the cost demands for oxide cathode materials are escalating and the reduction of costly Ni element content has become an inevitable trend due to increasing market pressures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0001_R_d264496983e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Mater">1</a>, <a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0002_R_d264496983e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Carbon Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the decrease of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox couple will unavoidably result in a capacity decline.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0003_R_d264496983e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Batteries Supercaps">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, regulating and promoting the redox reactions of other economic transition metal (TM) elements to alleviate the capacity deficit caused by reduced Ni content has emerged as a focal point for the progress of next-generation low-cost high-performance oxide cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0004_R_d264496983e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater. Acc. Chem. Res. Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>LOCs for SIBs often contain a high content of Mn, which functions to stabilize layered structure without engaging in charge compensation but can be further regulated to exhibit electrochemical activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0005_R_d264496983e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nat. Mater. eScience">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Activation of the Mn redox reaction (MRR) to enhance its contribution to capacity can effectively compensate for the capacity decrease resulting from the lack of Ni.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0006_R_d264496983e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. Small">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the activation of MRR and the appearance of Mn<sup>3+</sup> are always suppressed as much as possible in the previous study of LOCs for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0007_R_d264496983e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Sustain. Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is widely acknowledged that the activation of the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction induces a very severe Jahn-Teller effect, that the occupancy of one electron in the doubly degenerate e<sub>g</sub> level results in the local Jahn-Teller distortion with elongation of Mn─O bonds along one of the octahedral axes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0007_R_d264496983e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Sustain. Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The long range orbital ordering in the materials exhibiting Mn redox activity results in collinear Jahn-Teller ordering, leading to a pronounced cooperative Jahn-Teller effect and a significant volume change, which causes grievous structure deterioration and fast capacity decay.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0007_R_d264496983e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Sustain. Energy Storage Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0008_R_d264496983e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nature J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, Mn<sup>3+</sup> is susceptible to disproportionation reactions, further resulting in the dissolution of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and subsequent collapse of the layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0009_R_d264496983e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Sci.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the Jahn-Teller effect represents a fundamental challenge for LOCs exhibiting MRR activity and addressing the Jahn-Teller effect to stabilize MRR while maximizing the activation of MRR represents a pivotal concern in the study of Mn-based cathode materials with MRR activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0009_R_d264496983e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0010_R_d264496983e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Commun.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the conventional approach to address this issue typically involves introducing additional redox couples to reduce MRR activity and mitigate the impact of the Jahn-Teller effect or incorporating electrochemically inert ions to reinforce MnO6 octahedron and inactivate MRR, which limits capacity contribution from MRR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0010_R_d264496983e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Commun.">10</a>, <a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0011_R_d264496983e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J. Small J. Am. Chem. Soc. J. Electrochem. Soc. Mater. Horiz.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, a biphasic low-nickel oxide cathode P2/O3-type Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2/O3) exhibits reversible Mn4+/3+ redox reaction fully activated by Na vacancy and the weakened hybridization of O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) orbital. Because of the interlaced arrangement of P2-type and O3-type crystal domains, there is no long range Jahn-Teller ordering in the composite-phase oxide, and the Jahn-Teller effect resulting from the MRR does not lead to structural decay; instead, the restoring effect of MRR on the lattice structure renders phase transition more reversible, reducing internal stress accumulation and improving structural stability. Besides, the introduction of P2-type phase in the biphasic materials reduces migration energy barrier for sodium ions and the interface between P2-type domains and O3-type crystal domains restrict the spontaneous de-intercalation of sodium ions from the bulk phase to enhance air stability. Hence, P2/O3 exhibits the superhigh discharge capacity of 146.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, excellent cycling stability with a retention of 93.1% after 200 cycles at 1 C, outstanding kinetic performance of 80.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C, prominent air stability with no obvious change exposed to humid air for 48&nbsp;h and wider voltage windows adaptability of 2–4.2&nbsp;V and 1.5-4&nbsp;V. The work provides valuable insights for the regulation of MRR and the design of low-cost and high-performance oxide cathodes for sodium ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403955-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403955-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Utilizing cationic potentials for the prediction and design of material structures has emerged as a potent methodology.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0012_R_d264496983e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Science">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0001">1a</a>, the cationic potential of the designed materials Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and the representative materials O3-NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P2-NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is calculated to predict the phase. The position of Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is still located in the O3-type phase but near to the dividing line of P2-type and O3-type phase, which may conduce to the generation of a composite-phase material with O3-type phase as the primary phase. The structure schematic illustrations of P2- and O3-Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0001">1b,c</a>, and the phase formation energy is calculated by density functional theory (DFT). The nearly equivalent formation energy of the P2-type and O3-type phases despite the slightly lower formation energy of O3-type phase provides further confirmation that the composite phase with O3-type phase as the primary phase is more likely to be generated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0013_R_d264496983e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Energy Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The structural uncertainty may result in a reduction of the long range ordering within a multiphase structure, leading to the alternated generation of the P2-type phase and O3-type phase.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403955-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/036b5158-a3e5-47e6-8f61-ffdde3dcbc28/aenm202403955-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/036b5158-a3e5-47e6-8f61-ffdde3dcbc28/aenm202403955-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e3eb6f60-e942-49f8-9158-9e8a94d8cd07/aenm202403955-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/036b5158-a3e5-47e6-8f61-ffdde3dcbc28/aenm202403955-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403955-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403955" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Pre-calculation of the “cation potential” of the designed materials and representative materials. Structure schematic illustration and phase formation energy of b) P2-type and c) O3-type Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.32</sub>Mn<sub>0.45</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. d) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of O3, P2/O3 and P2 Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. e) Rietveld refinement pattern of P2/O3. f) Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images, g) transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images and h) corresponding FFT patterns of P2/O3. i) High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images and j) EDS of P2/O3. sXAS spectra of k) Mn L-edge, l) Fe L-edge and m) O K-edge spectra for O3, P2/O3 and P2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub> precursor was synthesized by a co-precipitation way, exhibiting good crystallinity and spherical morphology depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Then a solid-state synthesis method was employed to fabricate cathode materials with varying sodium contents (Na<sub>1.0</sub>, Na<sub>0.8</sub>, Na<sub>0.67</sub>), as detailed in the supporting information (SI). Their phase structure is first analyzed by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Rietveld refinement. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0001">1d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) depict the well-indexed Bragg peaks of NaNi<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> to the space groups of R-3&nbsp;m and P63/mmc, respectively, demonstrating the formation of O3-type and P2-type layered structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0014_R_d264496983e703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Sci. Bull.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Different from O3-NaNi<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (O3) and P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2), the Bragg peaks of Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can be linked to both P2-type and O3-type phases, although the peaks of P2-type phase is weaker, indicating the generation of a biphasic structure as predicted and designed. The refinement results of P2/O3-Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2/O3) shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0001">1e</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) further confirm the synthesis of a hybrid phase crystal structure, with the O3 phase becoming the predominant phase at 89.9%. Raman spectra also substantiate the presence of a layered structure for all samples displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping are employed to examine the morphology and element distribution of O3, P2/O3, and P2. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0001">1f</a>; Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5,S6</a>, O3, P2/O3, and P2 all exhibit a secondary spherical shape, uniform particle size with an approximate diameter of 3–5&nbsp;nm and even elements distribution. It is noteworthy that as the sodium content decreases, the material's surface becomes smoother, indicating a reduction in residual surface sodium content. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images and EDS mapping for P2/O3 depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0001">1g–j</a> reveal the existence and coalescence of bi-phase at the lattice scale. In P2/O3, a crystal spacing of 0.544&nbsp;nm and 0.566&nbsp;nm is observed, which is corresponding to the (003) plane in the O3 phase structure and the (002) plane in the P2 structure, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0015_R_d264496983e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Sci. Bull.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the corresponding fast Fourier transform patterns of P2(002) plane and O3(003) plane in P2/O3 are in strong concordance with the above results. The atomic arrangement of P2/O3 shows the interlaced arrangement and different orientation in P2-type phase domain and O3-type phase domain, demonstrating the MnO6 octahedron do not align with the O3-type phase and P2-type phase in terms of distortion direction, resulting in different collinear orientations of Jahn-Teller distortion for the O3-type domains and P2-type domains.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0007_R_d264496983e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Sustain. Energy Storage Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0016_R_d264496983e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Mater. Phys. Rev. B">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the long range Jahn-Teller ordering disappears and the Jahn-Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> can be reduced significantly.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS) in the total electron yield (TEY) mode were deployed to demonstrate the electronic structure and surface chemical state. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the C 1s XPS spectra of O3, P2/O3 and P2 suggest that the surface CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> linked to surface sodium residual decreases with the phase change from O3 to P2/O3 to P2, which can be attributed the low sodium content and the improved air stability of P2 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0017_R_d264496983e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mn L3-edge, Ni L3-edge, Fe L3-edge sXAS spectra and Mn 2p, Ni 2p, Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> XPS spectra are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0001">1k,l</a> and Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8–S11</a> (Supporting Information). In order to maintain electrical neutrality, the average valence state of transition metals must increase as the sodium content decreases. The transition from O3 to P2/O3 results in an increase in the concentration of manganese ions with a valence state of +4 and nickel ions with a valence state of +3, while the transition from P2/O3 to P2 leads to a further rise in Mn<sup>4+</sup> ion concentration with little change in Ni<sup>3+</sup> concentration, indicating that manganese element exhibits greater flexibility in terms of valence states and is more sensitive to maintaining electrical neutrality. Surprisingly, there is a reduction of iron ions valance state, leading to an increase in the concentration of Fe<sup>2+</sup>, which may be linked to the formation of P2-type phase material. The existence of a certain amount of Fe<sup>2+</sup> can significantly enhance intrinsic conductivity, which arises from the facile transformation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> to Fe<sup>3+</sup> in oxygen octahedron, akin to the higher conductivity exhibited by Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> compared to Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0018_R_d264496983e832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> O K-edge sXAS spectra of all samples depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0001">1m</a> indicate that the surface sodium carbonate content gradually diminishes as the sodium content decreases and the phase structure changes, further confirming the significance of P2-type phase in reducing residual sodium and enhancing air stability. Furthermore, a pronounced enhancement of hybridization for the O (2p)-TM (3d) orbitals is observed in the P2/O3 and P2 compared to O3, particularly with respect to the O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) hybrid orbitals. The enhancement of O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) orbitals hybridization benefits the stabilization of the MnO6 octahedron, but does not promote MRR activation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0019_R_d264496983e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Energy Storage Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite O3 having the weakest hybridization of O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) orbitals, it lacks sufficient Na vacancies during sodiation process, resulting in only a small amount of Mn<sup>3+</sup> generated in the sintering process reacting. P2 possesses abundant Na vacancies, yet its stronger hybridization of O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) orbitals limits MRR activity. In contrast, P2/O3 exhibits weakened hybridization of O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) orbitals and relatively sufficient vacancies, potentially maximizing MRR activation.</p>
         
         <p>The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of all samples as cathode and Na metal as anode in a coin cell at 0.1 C (1 C = 130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) are displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0002">2a–c</a>. The initial charge specific capacities of O3, P2/O3, and P2 are 114.8, 111.7, and 82.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively; while their initial discharge specific capacities are 107.8, 146.4, and 105.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The initial coulombic efficiencies (ICE) for these materials are 93.9%, 131.1%, and 128.5%. O3 shows a typical charge/discharge curve for O3-type Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>a</sub>Fe<sub>b</sub>Mn<sub>c</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with subsequent cycle curves maintaining consistent characteristics, while P2/O3 and P2 differ significantly from O3. In O3-type Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>a</sub>Fe<sub>b</sub>Mn<sub>c</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials, Ni and Fe exhibit electrochemical activity, enabling the maintenance of electrical neutrality during the desorption of sodium through oxidation-reduction reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0020_R_d264496983e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, Mn, which is relatively electrochemically inert, almost does not take part in the charge compensation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0021_R_d264496983e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Power Sources">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While in P2/O3, the initial charging curve closely resembles that of O3, while the discharging curve exhibits a relatively gentle slope below 2.4&nbsp;V with a specific capacity contribution about 40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which persists consistently throughout subsequent charge and discharge process. This suggests the activation of MRR beside Ni/Fe redox reaction in P2/O3.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0006_R_d264496983e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. Small">6</a>, <a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0022_R_d264496983e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Power Sources">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The initial charging curve of P2 exhibits a single slope region above 2.9&nbsp;V due to the absence of a phase transition in P2-type material below 4.0&nbsp;V; conversely, the initial discharging curve displays both a slope region below 2.4&nbsp;V and one above 2.9&nbsp;V, exhibiting the activation of MRR but weak activity compared with P2/O3, which is consistent with the above analysis of the weakened hybridization of O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) orbitals and relatively sufficient vacancies to fully activate MRR in P2/O3. The electrochemical behaviors of all samples are further explored by CV studies exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0002">2d–f</a>. The fairly small peak below 2.2&nbsp;V in the CV curve of O3 should be attributed to the participation of a tiny part of Mn<sup>3+</sup> contained in the O3 material in charge compensation. It's visible that the MRR below 2.4&nbsp;V of P2/O3 and P2 is activated compared with that of O3 and contribute more capacity in P2/O3 compared to P2.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403955-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/df2419c4-26a5-402b-aa4f-bd2a4af1bec2/aenm202403955-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/df2419c4-26a5-402b-aa4f-bd2a4af1bec2/aenm202403955-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c51bdf69-ba13-4791-a0b7-5ca2f4eddb03/aenm202403955-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/df2419c4-26a5-402b-aa4f-bd2a4af1bec2/aenm202403955-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403955-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403955" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of a) O3, b) P2/O3 and c) P2. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) profile at a 0.05&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> scan rate of (a) O3, (b) P2/O3 and (c) P2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to further investigate the electronic structure variations of Mn during charging and discharging, ex situ characterization was conducted. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, the Mn K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra of P2/O3 before cycling (pristine, P2/O3-P), first fully charged (P2/O3-C1) state at 4.0&nbsp;V, and first fully discharged (P2/O3-D1) at 2.0&nbsp;V demonstrates that the Mn K-edge shifts to a slightly higher energy region upon charging, suggesting the little partial Mn<sup>3+</sup> is oxidized to Mn<sup>4+</sup>. While during the discharging process, the energy shift is greater than that upon charging and the Mn K-edge energy of P2/O3-D1 is much lower than P2/O3-P, suggesting the valance state of P2/O3-D1 is much lower and the Mn4+/Mn3+ redox is fully activated during discharge to participate in the charge compensation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0023_R_d264496983e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nature">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To identify local environment variation of Mn, the Fourier-transformed extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0003">3c</a> is analyzed to study the changes in the scattering path lengths of Mn-O and second shell Mn-TM. During charging, the Mn─O bond exhibits no significant change, indicating that the chemical valence and local electronic structure of Mn remain relatively stable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0006_R_d264496983e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater. Small">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the Mn-TM bond undergoes a slight leftward shift, suggesting oxidation reactions for Ni and Fe. Upon discharging, the Mn─O bond notably lengthens beyond its initial state and full charged state, signifying a shift to a lower valence state for Mn and the occurrence of the Jahn-Teller effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0010_R_d264496983e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Commun.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simultaneously, the Mn─TM bond also lengthens even compared to its initial state, indicating reduction reactions for Ni and Fe as well as for Mn.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403955-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d0363cea-f54d-46a7-b679-0d00c4d6a669/aenm202403955-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d0363cea-f54d-46a7-b679-0d00c4d6a669/aenm202403955-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eb0cac26-77e9-470b-acfe-3904f9b14b75/aenm202403955-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d0363cea-f54d-46a7-b679-0d00c4d6a669/aenm202403955-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403955-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403955" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ XANES of Mn K-edge, b) enlarged plots and c) EXAFS for P2/O3. d) Ex situ Mn 2p XPS spectra of P2/O3 with an etching depth of 20&nbsp;nm. Ex situ sXAS of e) Mn L3-edge and f) O K-edge for P2/O3. g) Schematic illustration for MRR.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Due to hard X-rays’ probe depth of a few microns, they offer comprehensive sensitivity for the material system. While sodiation/desodiation prefer to occur at the surface, changes in valence at the surface may more directly reveal alterations in redox reactions. To examine the surface properties of the cathode materials, ex situ XPS tests with an etching depth of 20&nbsp;nm carried out to examine the charge compensation mechanism during the intercalation/deintercalation of sodium ions. As displayed in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12,S13</a> (Supporting Information), Ni and Fe take part in charge compensation and demonstrate reversibility. The variations in the Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> XPS spectra and the corresponding proportion of Mn<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup> during the initial cycle and subsequent charge process are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. In P2/O3-P, Mn<sup>3+</sup> accounts for 40.55% while Mn<sup>4+</sup> accounts for 59.45%; upon charging to 4.0&nbsp;V, all Mn is oxidized to Mn<sup>4+</sup>; upon discharging to 2.0&nbsp;V, the ratio of Mn<sup>3+</sup> increases to 67.04%, with a decrease in the ratio of Mn<sup>4+</sup> to 32.96%; upon recharging to 4.0&nbsp;V (P2/O3-C2), all Mn remains oxidized as Mn<sup>4+</sup>. This suggests that there is a reversible conversion between Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> during the P2/O3 charge-discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0024_R_d264496983e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additional, ex situ sXAS with a detection limit depth of ≈100&nbsp;nm in the TEY mode was utilized to further study the charge compensation mechanism of P2/O3 involving MRR. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0003">3e</a>, during the initial charging of P2/O3, trace amounts of Mn<sup>3+</sup> are oxidized to Mn<sup>4+</sup>, while during initial discharging, significant quantities of Mn<sup>4+</sup> are reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup> without detectable Mn<sup>2+</sup>. In the subsequent charging, they are reversibly oxidized back to Mn<sup>4+</sup>. This process conclusively demonstrates that P2/O3 undergoes a reversible Mn4+/3+ redox reaction beside Ni and Fe redox, consistent with the ex situ XPS and XAS results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0025_R_d264496983e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> O K-edge sXAS spectra displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0003">3f</a> indicate that throughout the initial charging and discharging, a notable alteration is observed in the pre-edge peaks corresponding to O (2p)-TM (3d-e<sub>g</sub>) and O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) hybridized band, particularly in P2/O3-D1 where the electrons in the pre-edge peak are concentrated in the O (2p)-TM (3d-e<sub>g</sub>) hybridized orbital. This further substantiates the robust correlation between O (2p)-TM (3d) hybridization and redox reactions, as well as underscores the advantageous role of weakened O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>) hybridization in activating the MRR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0019_R_d264496983e1079" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Energy Storage Mater. Chem. Eng. J.">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0025_R_d264496983e1079" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, there is also a certain degree of regularity emerged in the evolution of surface sodium carbonate: it is initially present but disappeared during charging, likely due to decomposition producing gas; reappeared during discharging; and then decomposed again during subsequent charging. The continuous generation of surface sodium carbonate may be fundamental to ongoing gas production and electrolyte decomposition throughout battery cycling. Thus, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0003">3g</a>, a portion of Mn<sup>4+</sup> ions will be reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions during the discharge process. As a result of the spontaneous elimination of degenerate energy levels, electrons occupy lower energy orbitals, leading to the lengthened Mn─O bonds along one axis in the MnO6 octahedra as well as subsequent structural distortion, which accelerating LOC degradation. Therefore, suppressing structural distortion caused by MnO6 octahedral distortion is the most crucial issue for stabilizing MRR.</p>
         
         <p>To further explore the structural changes of P2/O3 during charging and discharging, as well as the impact of the Mn4+/3+ redox reaction on the structure transformation, in situ XRD analysis was performed. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, the P2 phase persists throughout the entire cycle, whereas the O3 phase undergoes a reversible O3-P3 phase transformation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0026_R_d264496983e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Small Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the commencement of charging, as sodium ions continue to be released, the (002) and (004) peaks in the P2 phase, along with the (003) and (006) peaks in the O3 phase, gradually shift towards lower angles; simultaneously, the (101), (012), and (104) peaks in the O3 phase progressively shift towards higher angles. This indicates an expansion of c-axis spaces due to reduced shielding effect between adjacent negatively charged oxygen layers during sodium extraction and a contraction of the a/b axis driven by stronger TM─O bonding energy and smaller ionic radius resulting from an increase in valence state of reactive TM cations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0027_R_d264496983e1106" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the charging voltage reached 3.1&nbsp;V, a few new peaks corresponding to P3-type Phase emerge: (003), (006), (101), (012), and (015). The coexistence of phases P2+P3+O3 for a brief period is succeeded by a rapid attenuation and disappearance of the peaks corresponding to the O3 phase, followed by the coexistence of phases P2+P3 until the charging voltage reached 4.0&nbsp;V, which still performs the left shift of P3(003), P3(006) and P2(002) as well as the right shift of P3(101) and P3(012) but no obvious shift of P3(015). The phase transition observed during the discharge process is precisely the reverse, as sodium ions are inserted, leading to a gradual transformation of P2+P3 back into P2+O3. In the subsequent cycle, the reversible phase change reaction can still be observed. Thus, throughout the entire charge/discharge process, the phase transformation proceeds as follows: P2+O3-P2+P3+O3-P2+P3-P2+P3+O3-P2+O3.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403955-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2a8df7fe-362f-45af-88f8-95c2c8030ccf/aenm202403955-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2a8df7fe-362f-45af-88f8-95c2c8030ccf/aenm202403955-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5aa2df83-3140-4b15-9888-f907ef76b145/aenm202403955-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2a8df7fe-362f-45af-88f8-95c2c8030ccf/aenm202403955-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403955-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403955" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Contour map of in-situ XRD patterns for P2/O3. 3D Contour map of in situ XRD patterns for b) 15–16.5°, c) 21–38°, and d) 41–45°. TEM images of P2/O3 after (c) activation at 0.1 C and (d) 200 cycles at 1 C. e) Structure schematic illustration for no long range Jahn-Teller ordering of P2/O3. Capacity-voltage curves at 1 C of f) P2 and g) P2/O3 for different cycle number.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>It is noteworthy that the activation of MRR below 2.4&nbsp;V during the initial discharge does not induce the formation of a new phase. In contrast, sodium ions continue to intercalate into the P2+O3 phase and undergo layer sliding, leading to a right shift in the peaks. Moreover, this peak shift results in a more pronounced restoration of the crystal lattice over its original state, thereby suppressing the lattice expansion, stress accumulation, and structural degradation from the ongoing O3-P3 phase transition during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0027_R_d264496983e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the structural changes induced by the MRR not only do not exert any adverse impact on the phase transition but also exert a “restoring effect” on the material's crystal structure and enhance the material's structural stability. Besides, TEM image of P2/O3 after activation shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0004">4c</a>, when there plenty of Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the bulk and Jahn-Teller effect breaks out, suggests P2-type and O3-type phases are arranged in a disordered manner, resulting in a long-range disordered structure at the crystal domain scale. Consequently, the octahedra distortion of MnO6 cannot be effectively coordinated, which inhibits both crystal structure distortion and degradation, thereby ensuring the stability of the Mn redox couple and crystal structure. To further investigate the evolution of MRR during long-term cycling, cycling tests of O3, P2/O3 and P2 at 1 C after 3 cycles activation at 0.1 C were conducted presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). The discharge specific capacities of O3, P2/O3, and P2 at 1 C are 94.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 108.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and 91.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Following 200 cycles, the capacity retention stands at 77.1%, 93.1%, and 83.9% for each material, demonstrating the biphase stabilizes layered structure and enhances long-cycling stability of P2/O3. As can be seen from Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), the average discharge voltage decay of P2/O3 is significantly mitigated compared with O3, which can be ascribed to the existence of the P2 phase and the inhibition of irreversible transition metal migration within the composite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0028_R_d264496983e1158" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) for O3, P2/O3 and P2 cells after 200 cycles at 1 C shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information) suggests the lowest charge transfer impendence of P2/O3 and confirms its superior Na<sup>+</sup> transport efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0029_R_d264496983e1169" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), the XRD pattern of P2/O3 after cycling still exhibits distinct peaks linked to the P2-type phase and the O3-type phase, albeit with diminished peak intensities and a shift of the O3(003) peak towards lower angles. This suggests that despite minor structural decay, the integrity of the P2/O3 structure remains preserved. SEM images of P2/O3 after cycling shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) indicates that the morphology of P2/O3 remains unchanged during the cycling process, with no evidence of cracking. The XPS results of P2/O3 after cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information) reveals the generation of a cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) film on the surface, which is an organic-inorganic composite containing C-O, C = O, and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>, serving to protect P2/O3 from electrolyte corrosion. TEM image of P2/O3 after 200 cycles shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0004">4d</a> further proves that the composite phase of P2-type domains and O3-type domains in P2/O3 disrupt the long-range colinear Jahn-Teller ordering and mitigates cooperative Jahn-Teller distortion, thereby suppressing the Jahn-Teller effect and achieving excellent structural integrity with minimal volume changes during the MRR.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0007_R_d264496983e1190" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Sustain. Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although variations in grain size and coherent crystal planes can contribute to a reduction in long-range order, the range of ordered regions achieved through composite phase design tends to be smaller, which can more effectively mitigate the aggregation of MnO6 octahedra distortions and enhance structural stability.</p>
         
         <p>Therefore, a correlation between stabilized MRR and reduced long range order induced by composite phase can be delineated as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0004">4e</a>. In contrast with the pure O3-type and P2-type phase materials, there is no long range Jahn-Teller ordering in the biphasic P2/O3, which mitigates the cooperative distortion of the MnO6 octahedron induced by MRR, alleviates structural degradation associated with the Jahn-Teller effect, and consequently enhances the reversibility of MRR. To further support this observation, the discharge curve changes at different cycle number of O3, P2, and P2/O3 are analyzed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0004">4f,g</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). It is evident that discharge capacity below 2.4&nbsp;V is influenced by the MRR. As cycling progresses in O3, there is a gradual increase in the contribution of capacity below 2.4&nbsp;V but without a corresponding slope region due to structural deterioration and polarization aggravation rather than MRR-related factors. While in P2/O3, it doesn't exhibit fully active for MRR at the beginning, and as the cycling progressing, the MRR is fully activated at the 75<sup>th</sup> cycle and maintains a consistent contribution of approximately 12% to the discharge specific capacity. In contrast, the MRR in P2 displays persistent long-range Jahn-Teller distortion, leading to a rapid decrease in its contribution to specific capacity with cycle progression, indicating severe Jahn-Teller effect causing irreversible structural degradation. Furthermore, the comparison of transition metal dissolution of O3, P2/O3, and P2 after cycling is presented in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). It is observed that O3 exhibits a significant issue of Fe ion dissolution, whereas the dissolution of Fe ions for P2/O3 and P2 is mitigated. This suggests the existence of the P2-type phase can suppress irreversible migration of Fe<sup>4+</sup> and Fe ions dissolution, potentially accounting for the suppressed voltage decay in P2/O3 and P2.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0028_R_d264496983e1215" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, due to the activation of MRR in P2/O3 and P2, Mn ion dissolution presents a more severe problem than in O3; however, there is a notable inhibition of Mn ion dissolution in P2/O3. This also indicates that the constricted long range Jahn-Teller ordering in P2/O3 inhibits both Jahn-Teller effect and disproportionation reaction of Mn<sup>3+</sup>, thereby achieving structural stability. In addition, the study was conducted to improve the cut-off discharge voltage of P2/O3 to 2.4&nbsp;V to inactivate the MRR depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information), and it is found that the not only the discharge specific capacity but also capacity retention is significantly reduced (93.6% vs 99.4%, 100 cycles). This suggests that the participation of MRR in charge compensation inducing restoring effect on structure is advantageous for maintaining structural stability, thereby facilitating high capacity and prolonged lifespan of low-nickel oxide materials.</p>
         
         <p>Subsequently, the kinetic properties of P2/O3 were investigated. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0005">5a</a>, P2/O3 comprises P2-type phase and O3-type phase, leading to the existence of two sodium ion migration paths. The calculated sodium ion migration barriers in the P2- Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and O3-Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub> phase depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0005">5b,c</a>, unequivocally demonstrate that the sodium ions migration barrier in P2-type phase is markedly lower than O3-type phase. This indicates incorporating the P2-type phase into the P2/O3 structure can significantly enhance ion transport and improve kinetic properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0030_R_d264496983e1259" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Rate performance of all samples displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0005">5d,e</a>; Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23,S24</a> (Supporting Information) reveals that the dynamic properties of P2/O3 have been significantly enhanced in comparison to those of O3 and are approaching the level of the P2 phase, despite the fact that P2-type phase constitutes only 10.1% in P2/O3 and P2/O3 exhibits a high capacity of 80.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C (Vs 27.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for O3 and Vs 72.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for P2). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0005">5f</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information) show the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurement result of P2/O3. At the charging start, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient gradually decreases as charging progresses, reaching its nadir during the coexistence of the three phases. Subsequently, following the transformation from O3+P2 phase to P3+P2 phase, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient stabilizes at approximately 10<sup>−9.5</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>/s. During discharging, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient for the P2+P3 phase exhibits a slight increase; however, it experiences a sharp decline to its minimum point during the coexistence of three phases before sharply rising upon further transformation into the P2+O3 phase. This rapid reduction and recover of the sodium ion diffusion coefficient in the presence of multiphases (P2+P3+O3) is associated with the P/O interfaces beside the phase transformation of P3 to O3. The P/O interfaces can be classified into two types: interfaces with connected sodium ion channels and those without. The interfaces with connected sodium ion channels have negligible influence on sodium ion transport, while the interfaces without connected sodium ion channels decrease the migration rate of sodium ions. Consequently, an increase in the number of phases and phase interfaces during the phase transition process leads to a reduction in sodium ion migration rate; however, as the number of interfaces decreases, the migration rate of sodium ions is restored. Thereafter, as sodium ions intercalates, there is a gradual decrease in the sodium ion diffusion coefficient. In comparison with the relevant reports on O3-type materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0031_R_d264496983e1288" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano Small">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the diffusion coefficient of P2/O3 has been significantly enhanced, further illustrating the improvement in dynamic performance resulting from the introduction of the P2-type phase. To evaluate the structural stability and MRR's stability as well as the fast-charging performance, extensive long-term and fast charging-discharging electrochemical stability tests are conducted. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0005">5g,h</a>, P2/O3 displays excellent capacity retention, retaining 75.8% at 5 C after 1000 cycles and 71.7% at 10 C after 2000 cycles. It further demonstrates the exceptional structural stability and robustness of the MRR in P2/O3.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403955-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5cd9086c-6d84-4a44-b8a8-f00bfa1f019d/aenm202403955-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5cd9086c-6d84-4a44-b8a8-f00bfa1f019d/aenm202403955-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3dd94486-4cd3-4edb-9ff7-97de406a8482/aenm202403955-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5cd9086c-6d84-4a44-b8a8-f00bfa1f019d/aenm202403955-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403955-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403955" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Structure schematic illustration for P2/O3. Migration energy barriers in b) O3- and c) P2-Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.34</sub>Mn<sub>0.43</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. d) Rate capabilities of P2/O3. e) Coparision of rate performance for O3, P2/O3 and P2. f) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients from GITT calculation during the charge and discharge processes. Long cycling performance at g) 5 C and h) 10 C of P2/O3.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Subsequent investigations were carried out to assess P2/O3's application properties. Following exposure of P2/O3 to 25&nbsp;°C and 50% RH humidity for 12&nbsp;h, 24&nbsp;h, and 48&nbsp;h, no significant alterations are detected in the P2-type phase shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0006">6a,b</a>. However, slight shifts towards lower angles are observed in the (003) and (104) peaks of the O3-type phase without new hydrated phase peaks formation, indicating that P2/O3 maintained a high level of structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0032_R_d264496983e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of P2/O3 exposed for 12&nbsp;h, 24&nbsp;h and 48&nbsp;h are presented in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26–S28</a> (Supporting Information), showing that exposure of P2/O3 to air has a minimal impact on the charging and discharging curves and the capacity performance as well as the MRR. Cycling tests depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0006">6c</a> demonstrate that the capacity retention of P2/O3 after exposure to air for 12&nbsp;h, 24&nbsp;h, and 48&nbsp;h at 1 C after 100 cycles are 97.8%, 98.5%, and 98.1% respectively, further confirming the exceptional air stability and structural integrity of P2/O3 during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0033_R_d264496983e1350" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results of O3 and P2 after exposure to air for 48&nbsp;h are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> (Supporting Information). Although the air stability of P2 is superior to that of O3, it is still not as favorable as that of P2/O3. This indicates that the outstanding air stability of P2/O3 stems not only from the existence of P2-type phase but also from the significant role of the interface in improving air stability. When exposed in the humid air, the interfaces between P2-type domains and O3-type domains in P2/O3 will restrict the spontaneous de-intercalation of sodium ions from the bulk phase, reducing the formation of surface residual sodium layers and enhancing air stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403955-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2a0c5def-c530-43bf-bfdd-cffa76ee74ef/aenm202403955-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2a0c5def-c530-43bf-bfdd-cffa76ee74ef/aenm202403955-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/17204bf5-1b06-4cb4-bab3-5a4b63a1ef82/aenm202403955-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2a0c5def-c530-43bf-bfdd-cffa76ee74ef/aenm202403955-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403955-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403955" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern and b) enlarged selected peaks and c) electrochemical performance of P2/O3 exposed to air for different time at 50%RH and 25 °C. d) Galanostatic charge curves at 0.1 C and e) cycling performance at 1 C of P2/O3 with an ultrahigh mass loading of 15&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. f) Galanostatic charge curves at 0.1 C and g) cycling performance at 1 C, 1.5–4.0&nbsp;V of P2/O3. h) Galanostatic charge curves at 0.1 C and i) cycling performance at 1 C, 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V of P2/O3.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The high mass loading characteristic of P2/O3 is also investigated using the dry electrode preparation method. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0006">6d,e</a>, even when the P2/O3 mass loading reached 15&nbsp;mg/cm<sup>2</sup>, the discharge specific capacity remains as high as 143.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention of up to 85.7% after 300 cycles. This outstanding high mass loading characteristic can be attributed to the superior intrinsic electronic conductivity and excellent ions diffusion dynamics of P2/O3.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0034_R_d264496983e1390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Electrochem. Commun.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The wide voltage application property of P2/O3 with Mn participating in charge compensation are also investigated. A voltage range of 1.5-4&nbsp;V is chosen to fully activate the MRR shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0006">6f,g</a>, allowing for comprehensive utilization of the MRR. This leads to an ultrahigh discharge specific capacity up to 159.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a cycle retention of 84.6% at 1 C after 100 cycles. These results indicate that complete activation of the Mn4+/3+ redox reaction for charge compensation can significantly enhance capacity performance, although it may potentially result in some degree of structural degradation, thereby transforming the restoring effect on structure into a degradative effect. Therefore, careful consideration should be given to selecting the discharge cut-off voltage in order to strike a balance between capacity and cycling. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0006">6h</a> demonstrates the occurrence of the P3-O3' phase transformation under the voltage range of 2.4-4.2&nbsp;V, leading to a capacity rise of P2/O3 to 161.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. By comparison, the discharge specific capacity of O3 only increased to 133 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> within the same voltage range (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30a</a>, Supporting Information). As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403955-fig-0006">6i</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30b</a> (Supporting Information), after 100 cycles at 1 C, O3 exhibited a very low capacity retention of 5.1%, while that of P2/O3 is at an impressive level of 80%. This indicates that the interlocking effect from composite phase structure and restoring effect from MRR activation significantly suppress the P3-O3' phase transition and enhance the material's structural stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403955-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403955-bib-0014_R_d264496983e1418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Sci. Bull.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These findings fully demonstrate that designing a biphasic structure and activating MRR can effectively mitigate structural degradation during storage and cycling, improve high-rate performance, cycling stability, and stability, thereby showcasing excellent application characteristics for P2/O3.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403955-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403955-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the close formation energy of P2-type and O3-type phase results in no long range ordering structure and the formation of a composite phase cathode material P2/O3-type Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.32</sub>Mn<sub>0.45</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (P2/O3) with O3-phase as the predominant phase. Ex situ characterization reveals reversible transition of Mn<sup>4+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+</sup> during charging and discharging, which is activated by Na vacancies and weakened hybridization of O (2p)-TM (3d-t<sub>2</sub><sub>g</sub>), demonstrating Mn redox involvement in charge compensation. Because of the anisotropy of P2-type and O3-type crystal domains in P2/O3, the long-range Jahn-Teller ordering in the composite-phase material is constricted, significantly suppressing the Jahn-Teller effect induced by the MRR and thereby stabilizing Mn redox during cycling. Additionally, the MRR shows a restoring effect for phase transition, improving structural reversibility while suppressing structural degradation. The introduction of P2 phase in the biphasic materials also reduces migration energy barrier for sodium ions significantly. These characteristics enable the material to achieve an ultrahigh discharge specific capacity of 146.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, with a distinguished capacity retention as high as 93.1% after 200 cycles at 1 C and 71.7% after 2000 cycles at 10 C. Furthermore, P2/O3 exhibits excellent air stability, processing performance and wider voltage range adaptability. This study unveils new opportunities for regulating redox reactions to enhance the electrochemical performance of low-nickel or even nickel-free oxide cathode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
